        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <b>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">
            Anatomical terminology
           </a>
          </i>
         </b>
         <div style="text-align: right;">
          <small class="noprint">
           [
           <a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q169342" title="d:Q169342">

      <p>
       The
       <b>
        retina
       </b>
       is the third and inner coat of the eye which is a light-sensitive layer of tissue. The optics of the eye create an image of the visual world on the retina (through the
       <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">
        cornea
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">
        lens
       </a>
       ), which serves much the same function as the film in a camera. Light striking the retina initiates a cascade of chemical and electrical events that ultimately trigger nerve impulses. These are sent to various visual centres of the
       <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">
        brain
       </a>
       through the fibres of the
       <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">
        optic nerve
       </a>
       . Neural retina typically refers to three layers of neural cells (photo receptor cells, bipolar cells, and ganglion cells) within the retina, while the entire retina refers to these three layers plus a layer of pigmented epithelial cells.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In vertebrate
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryonic_development" title="Embryonic development">
        embryonic development
       </a>
       , the retina and the optic nerve originate as outgrowths of the developing brain, specifically the embryonic
       <a href="/wiki/Diencephalon" title="Diencephalon">
        diencephalon
       </a>
       ; thus, the retina is considered part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
        central nervous system
       </a>
       (CNS) and is actually brain tissue.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is the only part of the CNS that can be visualized
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasiveness_of_surgical_procedures#Non-invasive_procedure" title="Invasiveness of surgical procedures">
        non-invasively
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The retina is a layered structure with several layers of
       <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">
        neurons
       </a>
       interconnected by
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synapse" title="Chemical synapse">
        synapses
       </a>
       . The only neurons that are directly sensitive to light are the
       <a href="/wiki/Photoreceptor_cell" title="Photoreceptor cell">
        photoreceptor cells
       </a>
       . For vision, these are of two types:
       <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">
        rods
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">
        cones
       </a>
       . Rods function mainly in dim light and provide black-and-white vision while cones support the perception of colour. A third type of photoreceptor, the
       <a href="/wiki/Intrinsically_photosensitive_retinal_ganglion_cells" title="Intrinsically photosensitive retinal ganglion cells">
        photosensitive ganglion cells
       </a>
       , is important for
       <a href="/wiki/Entrainment_(chronobiology)" title="Entrainment (chronobiology)">
        entrainment
       </a>
       and reflexive responses to the brightness of light.
      </p>
      <p>
       Neural signals from the rods and cones undergo processing by other neurons of the retina. The output takes the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">
        action potentials
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
        retinal ganglion cells
       </a>
       whose
       <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">
        axons
       </a>
       form the optic nerve. Several important features of
       <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">
        visual perception
       </a>
       can be traced to the retinal encoding and processing of light. The term is from Latin
       <i>
        rēte
       </i>
       , meaning "net".
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14">
         <a href="#Additional_Images">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           7
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Additional Images
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15">
         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           8
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           See also
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           9
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           References
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17">
         <a href="#Further_reading">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           10
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Further reading
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18">
         <a href="#External_links">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           11
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           External links
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">
        Structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Retina&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray881.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="335" data-file-width="500" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gray881.png/300px-Gray881.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gray881.png/450px-Gray881.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/Gray881.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray881.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Section of retina
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The vertebrate retina has ten distinct layers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From closest to farthest from the vitreous body – that is, from closest to the front exterior of the head towards the interior and back of the head:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Inner_limiting_membrane" title="Inner limiting membrane">
         Inner limiting membrane
        </a>
        – basement membrane elaborated by
        <a href="/wiki/Muller_glia" title="Muller glia">
         Müller cells
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Nerve_fiber_layer" title="Nerve fiber layer">
         Nerve fibre layer
        </a>
        – axons of the
        <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
         ganglion cell
        </a>
        nuclei (note that a thin layer of Müller cell footplates exists between this layer and the inner limiting membrane)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ganglion_cell_layer" title="Ganglion cell layer">
         Ganglion cell layer
        </a>
        – contains nuclei of ganglion cells, the axons of which become the optic nerve fibres for messages and some displaced
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retina_amacrine_cell" title="Retina amacrine cell">
         amacrine cells
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Inner_plexiform_layer" title="Inner plexiform layer">
         Inner plexiform layer
        </a>
        – contains the synapse between the
        <a href="/wiki/Retina_bipolar_cell" title="Retina bipolar cell">
         bipolar cell
        </a>
        axons and the dendrites of the
        <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
         ganglion
        </a>
        and amacrine cells.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Inner_nuclear_layer" title="Inner nuclear layer">
         Inner nuclear layer
        </a>
        – contains the nuclei and surrounding cell bodies (perikarya) of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amacrine_cells" title="Amacrine cells">
         amacrine cells
        </a>
        , bipolar cells and
        <a href="/wiki/Retina_horizontal_cell" title="Retina horizontal cell">
         horizontal cells
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Outer_plexiform_layer" title="Outer plexiform layer">
         Outer plexiform layer
        </a>
        – projections of rods and cones ending in the rod spherule and cone pedicle, respectively. These make synapses with dendrites of bipolar cells.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macula" title="Macula">
         macular
        </a>
        region, this is known as the
        <i>
         Fiber layer of
         <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Gustav_Jakob_Henle" title="Friedrich Gustav Jakob Henle">
          Henle
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Outer_nuclear_layer" title="Outer nuclear layer">
         Outer nuclear layer
        </a>
        – cell bodies of rods and cones
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/External_limiting_membrane" title="External limiting membrane">
         External limiting membrane
        </a>
        – layer that separates the inner segment portions of the photoreceptors from their cell nucleus
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Layer_of_rods_and_cones" title="Layer of rods and cones">
         Layer of rods and cones
        </a>
        – layer of
        <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">
         rod cells
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">
         cone cells
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Retinal_pigment_epithelium" title="Retinal pigment epithelium">
         Retinal pigment epithelium
        </a>
        – single layer of cuboidal cells (with extrusions not shown in diagram). This is closest to the choroid.
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       <br/>
      </p>
      <p>
       These can be simplified into 4 main processing stages: photoreception, transmission to
       <a href="/wiki/Retina_bipolar_cell" title="Retina bipolar cell">
        bipolar cells
       </a>
       , transmission to
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
        ganglion cells
       </a>
       which also contain photoreceptors, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosensitive_ganglion_cell" title="Photosensitive ganglion cell">
        photosensitive ganglion cells
       </a>
       , and transmission along the optic nerve. At each synaptic stage there are also laterally connecting
       <a href="/wiki/Retina_horizontal_cell" title="Retina horizontal cell">
        horizontal
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retina_amacrine_cell" title="Retina amacrine cell">
        amacrine cells
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The optic nerve is a central tract of many axons of ganglion cells connecting primarily to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateral_geniculate_body" title="Lateral geniculate body">
        lateral geniculate body
       </a>
       , a visual relay station in the diencephalon (the rear of the forebrain). It also projects to the
       <a href="/wiki/Superior_colliculus" title="Superior colliculus">
        superior colliculus
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Suprachiasmatic_nucleus" title="Suprachiasmatic nucleus">
        suprachiasmatic nucleus
       </a>
       , and the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nucleus_of_the_optic_tract&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nucleus of the optic tract (page does not exist)">
        nucleus of the optic tract
       </a>
       . It passes through the other layers creating the
       <a href="/wiki/Optic_disc" title="Optic disc">
        optic disc
       </a>
       in primates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Additional structures, not directly associated with vision, are found as outgrowths of the retina in some vertebrate groups. In
       <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">
        birds
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Pecten_oculi" title="Pecten oculi">
        pecten
       </a>
       is a vascular structure of complex shape that projects from the retina into the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitreous_humour" title="Vitreous humour">
        vitreous humour
       </a>
       ; it supplies oxygen and nutrients to the eye, and may also aid in vision.
       <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">
        Reptiles
       </a>
       have a similar, but much simpler, structure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VB-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In adult humans, the entire retina is approximately 72% of a sphere about 22 mm in diameter. The entire retina contains about 7 million cones and 75 to 150 million rods. The optic disc, a part of the retina sometimes called "the blind spot" because it lacks photoreceptors, is located at the optic papilla, a nasal zone where the optic-nerve fibres leave the eye. It appears as an oval white area of 3 mm². Temporal (in the direction of the temples) to this disc is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macula" title="Macula">
        macula
       </a>
       . At its centre is the
       <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">
        fovea
       </a>
       , a pit that is responsible for our sharp central vision but is actually less sensitive to light because of its lack of rods. Human and non-human
       <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">
        primates
       </a>
       possess one fovea as opposed to certain bird species such as hawks who are bifoviate and dogs and cats who possess no fovea but a central band known as the visual streak
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . Around the fovea extends the central retina for about 6 mm and then the peripheral retina. The edge of the retina is defined by the
       <a href="/wiki/Ora_serrata" title="Ora serrata">
        ora serrata
       </a>
       . The length from one ora to the other (or macula), the most sensitive area along the horizontal
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Meridian_(eye)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Meridian (eye) (page does not exist)">
        meridian
       </a>
       is about 32 mm.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retina-diagram.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="743" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Retina-diagram.svg/400px-Retina-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Retina-diagram.svg/600px-Retina-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Retina-diagram.svg/800px-Retina-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Retina-diagram.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Rods, cones and nerve layers in the retina. The front (anterior) of the eye is on the left. Light (from the left) passes through several transparent nerve layers to reach the rods and cones (far right). A chemical change in the rods and cones send a signal back to the nerves. The signal goes first to the
         <a href="/wiki/Retina_bipolar_cell" title="Retina bipolar cell">
          bipolar
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Retina_horizontal_cell" title="Retina horizontal cell">
          horizontal cells
         </a>
         (yellow layer), then to the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retina_amacrine_cell" title="Retina amacrine cell">
          amacrine cells
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
          ganglion cells
         </a>
         (purple layer), then to the optic nerve fibres. The signals are processed in these layers. First, the signals start as raw outputs of points in the rod and cone cells. Then the nerve layers identify simple shapes, such as bright points surrounded by dark points, edges, and movement. (Based on a drawing by
         <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Ram%C3%B3n_y_Cajal" title="Santiago Ramón y Cajal">
          Ramón y Cajal
         </a>
         , 1911.)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In section the retina is no more than 0.5 mm thick. It has three layers of
       <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">
        nerve
       </a>
       cells and two of
       <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">
        synapses
       </a>
       , including the unique
       <a href="/wiki/Ribbon_synapse" title="Ribbon synapse">
        ribbon synapse
       </a>
       . The optic nerve carries the
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
        ganglion cell
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">
        axons
       </a>
       to the brain and the blood vessels that open into the retina. The ganglion cells lie outermost in the retina while the photoreceptive cells lie innermost. Because of this counter-intuitive arrangement, light must first pass through and around the ganglion cells and through the thickness of the retina, (including its capillary vessels, not shown) before reaching the rods and cones. However it does not pass through the
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_pigment_epithelium" title="Retinal pigment epithelium">
        retinal pigment epithelium
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Choroid" title="Choroid">
        choroid
       </a>
       (both of which are opaque).
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">
        white blood cells
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">
        capillaries
       </a>
       in front of the photoreceptors can be perceived as tiny bright moving dots when looking into blue light. This is known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Blue_field_entoptic_phenomenon" title="Blue field entoptic phenomenon">
        blue field entoptic phenomenon
       </a>
       (or Scheerer's phenomenon).
      </p>
      <p>
       Between the
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
        ganglion cell
       </a>
       layer and the rods and cones there are two layers of
       <a href="/wiki/Neuropil" title="Neuropil">
        neuropils
       </a>
       where synaptic contacts are made. The neuropil layers are the
       <a href="/wiki/Outer_plexiform_layer" title="Outer plexiform layer">
        outer plexiform layer
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Inner_plexiform_layer" title="Inner plexiform layer">
        inner plexiform layer
       </a>
       . In the outer the rods and cones connect to the vertically running
       <a href="/wiki/Retina_bipolar_cell" title="Retina bipolar cell">
        bipolar cells
       </a>
       , and the horizontally oriented
       <a href="/wiki/Retina_horizontal_cell" title="Retina horizontal cell">
        horizontal cells
       </a>
       connect to ganglion cells.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg/300px-Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg/450px-Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg/600px-Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_photoreceptor_distribution.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Distribution of rods and cones along a line passing through the fovea and the blind spot of a human eye
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
          <a href="#cite_note-8">
           [8]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConeMosaics.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="600" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/ConeMosaics.jpg/300px-ConeMosaics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/ConeMosaics.jpg/450px-ConeMosaics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/ConeMosaics.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ConeMosaics.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Illustration of the distribution of cone cells in the fovea of an individual with normal color vision (left), and a color blind (protanopic) retina. Note that the center of the fovea holds very few blue-sensitive cones.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The central retina is cone-dominated and the peripheral retina is rod-dominated. In total there are about seven million cones and a hundred million rods. At the centre of the macula is the foveal pit where the cones are smallest and in a hexagonal mosaic, the most efficient and highest density. Below the pit the other retina layers are displaced, before building up along the foveal slope until the rim of the fovea or
       <a href="/wiki/Parafovea" title="Parafovea">
        parafovea
       </a>
       which is the thickest portion of the retina. The macula has a yellow pigmentation from screening pigments and is known as the macula lutea. The area directly surrounding the fovea has the highest density of rods converging on single bipolars. Since the cones have a much lesser power of merging signals, the fovea allows for the sharpest vision the eye can attain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Though the rod and cones are a mosaic of sorts, transmission from receptors to bipolars to
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
        ganglion cells
       </a>
       is not direct. Since there are about 150 million receptors and only 1 million optic nerve fibres, there must be convergence and thus mixing of signals. Moreover, the horizontal action of the
       <a href="/wiki/Retina_horizontal_cell" title="Retina horizontal cell">
        horizontal
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retina_amacrine_cell" title="Retina amacrine cell">
        amacrine cells
       </a>
       can allow one area of the retina to control another (e.g. one stimulus inhibiting another). This inhibition is key to the sum of messages sent to the higher regions of the brain. In some lower vertebrates (e.g. the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">
        pigeon
       </a>
       ), there is a "centrifugal" control of messages – that is, one layer can control another, or higher regions of the brain can drive the retinal nerve cells, but in primates this does not occur.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_2-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Development">
        Development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Retina&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Retinal development begins with the establishment of the eye fields mediated by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonic_hedgehog" title="Sonic hedgehog">
        Shh
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Six3&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Six3 (page does not exist)">
        Six3
       </a>
       with subsequent development of the optic vesicles via
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pax6" title="Pax6">
        Pax6
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lhx2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lhx2 (page does not exist)">
        Lhx2
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heavner_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Heavner-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The role of Pax6 in eye development was elegantly demonstrated by Walter Gehring and colleagues, who showed that ectopic expression of Pax6 can lead to eye formation on Drosophila antennae, wings, and legs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The optic vesicle gives rise to three structures: the neural retina, the retinal pigmented epithelium, and the optic stalk. The neural retina contains the retinal progenitor cells (RPCs) that give rise to the seven cell types of the retina. Differentiation begins with the
       <a href="/wiki/Retinal_ganglion_cell" title="Retinal ganglion cell">
        retinal ganglion cells
       </a>
       and concludes with production of the Muller glia. Although each cell type differentiates from the RPCs in a sequential order, there is considerable overlap in the timing of when individual cell types differentiate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heavner_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Heavner-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cues that determine a RPC daughter cell fate are coded by multiple transcription factor families including the
       <a href="/wiki/Basic_helix-loop-helix" title="Basic helix-loop-helix">
        bHLH
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Homeobox" title="Homeobox">
        homeodomain
       </a>
       factors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to guiding cell fate determination, cues exist in the retina to determine the Dorsal/Ventral and Nasal/Temporal axes. The D-V axis is established by a ventral to dorsal gradient of
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vax2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vax2 (page does not exist)">
        Vax2
       </a>
       , whereas the N-T axis is coordinated by expression of the forkhead transcription factors
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FOXD1" title="FOXD1">
        FOXD1
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/FOXG1" title="FOXG1">
        FOXG1
       </a>
       . Additional gradients are formed within the retina that aid in proper targeting of RGC axons that function to establish the retinotopic map.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heavner_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Heavner-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_supply">
        Blood supply
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Retina&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Blood supply">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-multiple_issues compact-ambox" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
         </div>
        </td>
